The Three Types of Conventional Signification
Author classifies conventional signification into three types.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish the three types of conventional signification (dalala wad'iyya) and correctly classify examples from Islamic texts.

Core Idea
A word can signify its whole meaning, a part, or a necessary external concomitant.

Understanding how a word conveys meaning is essential for interpreting legal and theological texts. The scholars of usul and logic classified signification into three categories: mutabaqa (full correspondence), tadammun (inclusion), and iltizam (concomitance). This lesson will help you identify each type in Arabic usage, sharpening your analytical skills when reading authoritative works.

Guided Path
1
Identify the word and its full meaning
For any given word, first determine its complete, conventionally agreed meaning. For example, 'house' (bayt) signifies the entire building. This is dalala mutabaqa.
The full meaning is what the word was coined to express.
2
Recognize partial signification
If the word signifies only a component of that full meaning, it is dalala tadammun. For 'house', signifying 'roof' or 'wall' is tadammun because those are parts of the whole meaning.
Tadammun requires that the part is intrinsic to the defined meaning.
3
Distinguish external concomitance
If the word signifies something necessarily linked by the mind but not part of the meaning, it is dalala iltizam. For 'house', signifying 'shelter' or 'owner' is iltizam, as these are not parts but necessary concomitants.
The connection must be mental (bi-'aql), not merely customary.
4
Apply the framework to Islamic terminology
Take the word 'salat' (prayer). Its full meaning (mutabaqa) is the specific set of actions and words. Signifying 'ruku' (bowing) is tadammun. Signifying 'thawab' (reward) is iltizam, because reward is a necessary consequence of valid prayer.
This classification helps avoid confusion when deriving rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
أَنْواعُ الدَّلَالَةِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ (٢٤) دَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى مَا وَافَقَهُ يَدْعُونَهَا: «دَلَالَةَ المُطَابَقَهُ» (٢٥) وَجُزْئِهِ: «تَضَمُّناً»، وَمَا لَزِمْ فَهُوَ: «الْتِزَامُ» إِنْ بِعَقْلِ الْتَزَمْ
Types of conventional signification: (24) The signification of a word for what it fully matches (its whole meaning) they call 'dalala al-mutabaqa' (correspondence). (25) And for its part: 'tadammun' (inclusion), and what is implied (necessarily) is 'iltizam' (concomitance) if it is necessitated by the mind.
Source pages p. 28

Commentary
This passage explains the three types of signification of a word. When a word signifies its entire meaning (e.g., 'house' signifies the whole building), it is dalala mutabaqa. When it signifies only a part of that meaning (e.g., 'house' signifies the roof), it is dalala tadammun. When it signifies something necessarily connected to the meaning (e.g., 'house' signifies the roof as necessary? Actually, 'roof' is a part, so tadammun. Iltizam is something like 'sun' signifies 'daylight' because it is necessarily implied by the mind. So iltizam is an external necessary implication.

Key Terms
دلالة المطابقة
دلالة التضمن
دلالة الالتزام

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the word 'masjid' (mosque) signifies 'place of prostration' (its full meaning), which type of signification is that?
A) Mutabaqa
B) Tadammun
C) Iltizam
D) None

Key Takeaway
Conventional signification is threefold: mutabaqa for the whole, tadammun for a part, and iltizam for an external necessary concomitant.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify the type of signification for the word 'zakat' when used to mean 'purification' (mutabaqa), 'portion of wealth' (tadammun), and 'blessing' (iltizam). Provide one Islamic example for each.
?
Analyze the hadith 'al-a'malu bi-nniyyat' (deeds are by intentions). Classify the signification of 'niyya' (intention) in this context: is it mutabaqa, tadammun, or iltizam? Explain.

Distinguishing Al-Kull, Al-Kulliyya, Al-Juz', Al-Juz'iyya
Author defines al-kull, al-kulliyya, al-juz', al-juz'iyya in context of judgment

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between al-kull (totality) and al-kulliyya (universality) and between al-juz' (part) and al-juz'iyya (particularity) in quantified propositions.

Core Idea
The whole judges the aggregate; universality judges each individual.

In logic, the way a judgment covers its subject is critical. This passage from al-Bajuri defines four key terms: al-kull (totality) applies to the group as a whole, al-kulliyya (universality) applies to every member individually, al-juz'iyya (particularity) applies to some, and al-juz' is the individual part. Understanding these distinctions will help you parse logical propositions precisely.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject of judgment
For each term, note what the judgment is about: the aggregate (majmu'), every individual (kull fard), some individuals (ba'd), or the part itself.
2
Contrast al-kull and al-kulliyya
Al-kull is about the set as a whole; al-kulliyya distributes to each individual. Example: 'The army is strong' (al-kull) vs. 'Every soldier is strong' (al-kulliyya).
3
Apply the distinction to a proposition
Take 'Every Muslim believes in Allah.' This is al-kulliyya, because it applies to each individual Muslim.
4
Define al-juz' and al-juz'iyya
Al-juz' is an individual member; al-juz'iyya is a judgment about some members. Example: 'Some Muslims are scholars' is al-juz'iyya.
5
Practice with a Fiqh example
Consider 'Alcohol is prohibited.' Is this al-kull (alcohol as a category) or al-kulliyya (every type of alcohol)? Usually it's al-kull: the class as a whole is prohibited, but the ruling applies to every individual, so it could be al-kulliyya. This nuance matters in usul al-fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَصْلٌ فِي الكُلِّ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْءِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ گ: كُلُّ ذَاكَ لَيْسَ ذَا وُقُوعِ (۳۷) «الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ (۳۸) وَحَيْتُمَا لِكُلِّ فَرْدٍ حُكِمَا فَإِنَّهُ: «كُلِّيَّةٌ قَدْعُلِمَا (۳۹) وَالحُكْمُ لِلْبَعْضِ هُوَ : «الجُزْثِيَّة) وَالجُزْءُ مَعْرِفَتُهُ جَلِيَّة
A section on al-kull, al-kulliyya, al-juz', and al-juz'iyya. (37) Al-kull is our judgment on the aggregate. (38) And whenever judgment is made for every individual, then it is known as 'al-kulliyya'. (39) And judgment for some is 'al-juz'iyya', and the part its knowledge is clear.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage defines four related terms in the context of judgment (ḥukm). Al-kull (the whole/totality) is defined as a judgment covering an entire group or set, e.g., 'All humans are mortal' - the judgment applies to the aggregated group. Al-kulliyya (universality) is the judgment that applies to every single individual, e.g., 'Every human is an animal' - it distributes over each. Al-juz'iyya (particularity) is the judgment that applies only to some individuals, e.g., 'Some humans are scholars'. Al-juz' (part) is simply an individual member of a set, and its meaning is clear. This distinction is foundational for understanding quantified propositions in logic.

Key Terms
الكل
الكلية
الجزء
الجزئية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
The statement 'The human race is mortal' is an example of:
A) Al-kull
B) Al-kulliyya
C) Al-juz'
D) Al-juz'iyya

Key Takeaway
Al-kull judges the group as a whole, al-kulliyya judges each individual, al-juz'iyya judges some, and al-juz' is the part itself.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify whether each of the following propositions is al-kull, al-kulliyya, al-juz'iyya, or simply a part (al-juz'): 1) 'The human race is mortal' (al-kull); 2) 'Every Muslim must pray' (al-kulliyya); 3) 'Some animals are halal' (al-juz'iyya).
?
Construct a Fiqh example of your own that illustrates the difference between al-kull and al-kulliyya. For instance, state a ruling about 'prayer' as a totality and then state it distributively.

The Six Types of Indication in Logic
Author enumerates six types of indication based on basis and medium

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the six types of dalalah based on their basis (convention, intellect, custom) and medium (verbal, non-verbal).

Core Idea
Indication is either conventional, intellectual, or customary, and each can be verbal or non-verbal — giving six types.

Before you can analyse arguments in Islamic sciences, you must understand how signs convey meaning. This passage gives a complete taxonomy of indication (dalalah) used across logic, usul al-fiqh, and kalam. Memorising these six types will help you identify what kind of evidence is at work in any given proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two criteria
Notice that the passage splits indication by BASIS (wad'iyya, 'aqliyya, 'adiyya) and by MEDIUM (lafz, ghayr lafz). This gives 2 x 3 = 6 types.
The basis tells us why the sign indicates; the medium tells us what carries the sign.
2
Memorize the six combinations
Write out a 3x2 grid: rows for basis, columns for medium. Fill in the examples from the text for each cell.
Example: (wad'iyya + lafz) = 'asad' for lion; (wad'iyya + ghayr lafz) = head nod for 'yes'.
3
Associate each with the given example
For each type, recall the specific example from the passage. Say it aloud in Arabic and English.
This builds strong recall and connects theory to concrete cases.
4
Test yourself with Islamic science examples
Think of one new example from usul al-fiqh or kalam for each type. For instance, the intellectual non-verbal type is the classic kalam argument: change of creation indicates huduth.
This ensures you can apply the classification beyond the text's own examples.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
هَذَا ؛ وَلِلدَّلَالَةِ سِتَّةُ أَقْسَامِ؛ لِأَنَّهَا إِمَّا وَضْعِيَّةٌ، أَوْ عَقْلِيَّةٌ، أَوْ عَادِيَّةٌ، وَعَلَى كُلِّ الدَّالُ : إِمَّا لَفْظُ، أَوْ غَيْرُهُ.
(١) - فَدَلَالَةُ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ كَـ : دَلَالَةِ الأَسَدِ عَلَى الحَيَوَانِ المُفْتَرِسِ .
(٢) - وَالعَقْلِيَّةِ : دَلَالَةِ اللَّفْظِ عَلَى وُجُودِ لَافِظهِ، أَوْ حَيَاتِهِ.
(٣) - وَالعَادِيَّةِ كَـ : دَلَالَةِ أَخْ - بِفَتْحِ الهَمْزَةِ، وَبِالخَاءِ المُعْجَمَةِ - عَلَى الوَجَعِ مُطلَقاً، و«أح» - بِضَمِّ الهَمْزَةِ وَفَتْحِهَا ، وَبِالحَاءِ المُهْمَلَةِ - عَلَى وَجَعِ الصَّدْرِ .
(٤) - وَدَلَالَةُ غَيْرِ اللَّفْظِ الوَضْعِيَّةِ كَ : دَلَالَةِ الإِشَارَةِ بِالرَّأْسِ إِلَى أَسْفَلِ عَلَى مَعْنَى نَعَمْ ، وَإِلَى أَعْلَى عَلَى مَعْنَى «لا».
(٥) - وَالعَقْلِيَّةِ كَـ : دَلَالَةِ تَغَيُّرِ العَالَمِ عَلَى حُدُوثِهِ.
(٦) - وَالعَادِيَّةِ كَـ : دَلَالَةِ الحُمْرَةِ عَلَى الخَجَلِ - أَي : الحَياءِ، وَالصُّفْرَةِ عَلَى الوَجَلِ - أي : الخَوْفِ.
This; and indication has six divisions: because it is either conventional, intellectual, or customary. And in each case the indicator: either a word or other than a word.
(1) - Indication of the word by convention: like the indication of 'lion' on the predatory animal.
(2) - And intellectual indication: the indication of the word on the existence of its speaker, or his life.
(3) - And customary indication: like the indication of 'akh' (with fatha on hamza and kha with 'ijam) on pain absolutely, and 'ah' (with damm or fatha on hamza and ha with 'ihmal) on chest pain.
(4) - And indication of non-word by convention: like the indication of pointing the head downwards on the meaning of 'yes', and upwards on the meaning of 'no'.
(5) - And intellectual indication: like the indication of the change of the world on its temporal origination.
(6) - And customary indication: like the indication of redness on shyness i.e. modesty, and pallor on fear i.e. fright.
Source pages p. 167

Commentary
This passage classifies indication (dalalah) into six types based on two criteria: the basis of the indication (whether it is by convention [wad'], by intellect ['aql], or by custom ['ada]) and the medium of the indicator (whether it is a word [lafz] or something other than a word [ghayr lafz]).
1. The first type is conventional indication of a word, exemplified by the word 'asad' (lion) indicating the predatory animal. This is the standard case of language.
2. The second is intellectual indication of a word, where hearing the word leads the intellect to infer the existence or life of the speaker.
3. The third is customary indication of a word, like the groan 'akh' indicating general pain and 'ah' indicating chest pain, due to habitual association.
4. The fourth is conventional indication by non-word, such as nodding the head down for 'yes' and up for 'no'.
5. The fifth is intellectual indication by non-word, for example, observing the change of the world leads the intellect to conclude that it is temporally originated (huduth), a key argument in kalam.
6. The sixth is customary indication by non-word, like redness of the face indicating shyness and paleness indicating fear.

Key Terms
الدلالة الوضعية
الدلالة العقلية
الدلالة العادية
اللفظ
غير اللفظ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of 'aqliyya (intellectual) indication involving a non-word?
A) The word 'asad' for the predatory animal.
B) The redness of the face indicating shyness.
C) The change of the world indicating its temporal origination (huduth).
D) Pointing downwards indicating 'yes'.

Key Takeaway
The classification of dalalah into six types is a fundamental tool in Islamic epistemology, showing that signs can indicate meanings through convention, intellect, or custom, in verbal or non-verbal forms.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?

The Logical Whole: Judgment or Collection?
Author defines 'whole' with commentary on literal/metaphorical uses and negative quantifiers.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between literal and metaphorical uses of 'whole' (al-kull) in Islamic logical propositions and correctly interpret its meaning in negative statements.

Core Idea
'Whole' in logic is not the judgment but the collection judged, with a shift in meaning when negated.

This passage from the logic textbook defines 'whole' as 'our judgment on the collection,' but the commentary immediately refines it: the true whole is the collection itself, not the judgment. This teaches us to watch for technical terms that have shifted from their original literal meaning. The commentary also introduces a crucial distinction between literal usage (applying to every individual) and metaphorical usage (applying to the group collectively), and how negation changes the logical force.

Guided Path
1
1. Identify the definiendum and the definition
The matn states: 'The whole: our judgment on the collection.' Note that the definiendum is 'al-kull' and the definition is phrased as a judgment (hukm).
In logic, definitions are often reworked by commentators.
2
2. Understand the commentator's correction
The commentary explains that the real whole is the subject (the collection judged), and calling the judgment 'whole' is a metaphor—naming a thing by its adjunct. Over time, this metaphorical usage became a conventional technical term.
This is common in Islamic sciences: terms evolve from literal to technical.
3
3. Distinguish literal vs. metaphorical 'on the collection'
The phrase 'on the collection' can mean either: (a) all individuals together (literal), as in 'eight angels' (Quran 69:17) where every individual is counted; or (b) some individuals collectively (metaphorical), as in 'the people of al-Azhar are scholars' where not every individual is a scholar, but the group as a whole is described.
The literal usage is the default; metaphorical usage requires context.
4
4. Apply the rule to affirmative vs. negative propositions
In affirmative statements, 'whole' implies all individuals. In negative statements (e.g., 'You did not give the whole ten'), it negates the entire collection, but does not negate every part—some individuals may still have been given.
This nuance is critical for understanding logical quantifiers in Islamic texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في الكل والكلية والجزء والجزئية
الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ
قَوْلُهُ: (فَصْل فِي الكُلِّ وَالكُلِّيَّةِ وَالجُزْءِ وَالجُزْئِيَّةِ) وَشَارَكَ الأَوَّلَيْنِ فِي البَدَاءَةِ بِـ «الكَافِ»: الكُلّيُّ، وَالأَخِيرَيْنِ فِي البَدَاءَةِ بـ «الجيم»: «الجُزْنِيُّ، فَجُمْلَةُ الأَلْفَاظِ سِتَّةٌ: ثَلَاثَةٌ مَبْدُقَةٌ بِالكَافِ، وَثَلَاثَةٌ مَبْدُقَةٌ بِالجِيمِ. قَوْلُهُ: («الكُلُّ : حُكْمُنَا ... إلخ) الكُلُّ فِي الحَقِيقَةِ هُوَ: المَوْضُوعُ الَّذِي هُوَ المَجْمُوعُ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ، فَتَسْمِيَةُ الحُكْمِ: «كُلًّا مِنْ بَابِ تَسْمِيَةِ الشَّيْء بِاسْمِ مُتَعَلَّقِهِ، لَكِنْ هَذَا بِاعْتِبَارِ الأَصْلِ، وَإِلَّا فَقَدْ صَارَ حَقِيقَةٌ اصْطِلَاحِيَّةٌ، كَمَا ذَكَرَهُ المَلَّوِيُّ فِي كَبِيرِهِ. قَوْلُهُ: عَلَى المَجْمُوعِ) أَيْ: عَلَى الأَفْرَادِ المُجْتَمِعِ جَمِيعِهَا، كَمَا هُوَ الحَقِيقَةُ فِي إِطْلَاقِ المَجْمُوعِ أَوْ عَلَى بَعْضِ الأَفْرَادِ المُجْتَمِعَةِ كَمَا هُوَ المَجَازُ فِيهِ. فَالأَوَّلُ؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِهِ تَعَالَى: ﴿وَعَيْلُ عَرْشَ رَبِّكَ فَوْقَهُمْ يَوْمَذٍ تَنِيَّةٌ﴾ [الحاقة: ١٧]. وَالثَّانِي؛ كَمَا فِي قَوْلِكَ أَهْلُ الأَزْهَرِ عُلَمَاءُ. قَوْلُهُ: (كَمَا هُوَ الحَقِيقَةُ فِي إِطْلَاقِ المَجْمُوعِ ... إلخ) هَذَا حُكْمُ الكُلِّ فِي الإِيجَابِ، أَمَّا فِي السَّلْبِ فَهُوَ النَّفْيُ عَنِ المَجْمُوعِ؛ كَقَوْلِنَا: مَا أَعْطَيْتَ كُلَّ العَشْرَةِ، فَلَا يُنَافِي الثَّبُوتَ فِي الْبَعْضِ، ذَكَرَهُ شَيْخُنَا العَدَوِيُّ. اهـ صَبَان.
Section on the whole, wholeness, part, and partiality. The whole: our judgment on the collection. His saying: 'Section on the whole, wholeness, part, and partiality' – the first two share the beginning with 'kaf': 'kulli', and the last two share the beginning with 'jim': 'juz'i'. So the total words are six: three beginning with 'kaf' and three beginning with 'jim'. His saying: ('The whole: our judgment ... etc.) The whole in reality is the subject that is the collection judged upon, so naming the judgment 'whole' is of the category of naming a thing by the name of its adjunct, but this is with respect to the original, otherwise it has become a conventional reality, as al-Mallawi mentioned in his larger work. His saying: 'on the collection' i.e., on all the individuals together, as is the reality in the usage of the collection, or on some of the individuals together as is the metaphorical usage. The first is as in His saying: 'And the angels of your Lord will be above them on that Day, eight' [Quran 69:17]. The second is as in your saying: 'The people of al-Azhar are scholars.' His saying: ('As it is the reality in the usage of the collection ... etc.) This is the rule of the whole in affirmative propositions; as for negative, it is negation of the collection, as in our saying: 'You did not give the whole ten', so it does not contradict affirmation of some, mentioned by our teacher al-‘Adawi. End. [From] Sabban.
Source pages p. 214

Commentary
This passage is a definitional section on the logical term 'whole' (al-kull). The matn defines 'whole' as 'our judgment on the collection', but the commentary immediately clarifies that technically the 'whole' is the subject of the judgment (the collection itself), not the judgment. The judgment is called 'whole' metaphorically, although in conventional usage it has become a technical term for the judgment. The commentary then explains that 'on the collection' can mean either all individuals together (literal) or some individuals collectively (metaphorical). Two examples are given: the Quranic verse 'and the angels of your Lord will be above them on that Day, eight' (Q 69:17) illustrates literal use where the number eight applies to the entire collection of angels; the statement 'The people of al-Azhar are scholars' illustrates metaphorical use where it is understood that not every individual is a scholar, but the group as a whole. Finally, the commentary notes an important nuance: in affirmative propositions, 'whole' implies all individuals, but in negative propositions (e.g., 'You did not give the whole ten'), it means negation of the entire group, which does not preclude the possibility that some individuals were given. This distinction is crucial for understanding logical quantifiers.

Key Terms
الكل
مجموع
حكم
حقيقة اصطلاحية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the proposition 'ما أعطيت كل العشرة' (You did not give the whole ten), what does it imply about giving some of the ten?
A) It implies that none were given.
B) It implies that some were given.
C) It neither affirms nor denies that some were given.
D) It implies that exactly five were given.

Key Takeaway
The logical term 'whole' in Islamic logic refers primarily to the collection judged, not the judgment itself, and its interpretation in negative statements does not negate every part.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the Quranic verse ﴿وَلَقَدْ نَعْلَمُ أَنَّهُمْ يَقُولُونَ إِنَّمَا يُعَلِّمُهُ بَشَرٌ﴾ (16:103). The disbelievers say 'a man teaches him.' Does the 'man' refer to a specific individual (literal) or the group? Use the concept of literal vs metaphorical 'whole' to assess.
?
In a fiqh ruling: 'لا تسلموا مفتاح المسجد لكل أحد' (Do not hand the mosque key to every single person). What does the negation imply? Can it be handed to some? Explain using the rule for negative quantifiers.

The Ten Categories

The Four Types of Categorical Propositions
Commentator lists and defines the four types of categorical propositions: individual, indefinite, universal, particular.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and classify any given categorical proposition as shakhsiyya (individual), muhmala (indefinite), kulliyya (universal), or juz'iyya (particular) based on its quantifier and subject specification.

Core Idea
The quantifier (or its absence) determines the logical type of a proposition.

In this lesson, we explore the fourfold division of categorical propositions as explained by al-Bajuri. Understanding these types is crucial for correctly interpreting premises in arguments, especially in Islamic legal and theological reasoning. By learning to identify whether a proposition is individual, indefinite, universal, or particular, you will sharpen your ability to evaluate the scope of claims.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject
Look at the subject term. Is it a specific individual (like 'Zayd'), a universal term with a quantifier, or a universal term without a quantifier?
For example, 'Zayd' is a specific individual; 'human' is a universal term.
2
Check for quantifier words
If there is a quantifier like 'every' (كل) or 'some' (بعض) or 'none' (لا شيء), note it. If no quantifier, the proposition is indefinite or possibly natural.
The universal quantifier 'every' indicates a universal proposition; 'some' indicates a particular.
3
Classify by type
If the subject is a specific individual, it is shakhsiyya. If the subject is universal with no quantifier, it is muhmala. If quantified by 'every' or 'none', it is kulliyya. If quantified by 'some' or 'not all', it is juz'iyya.
Remember that propositions about God should not be called shakhsiyya to avoid implying physical individuality.
4
Special case: natural propositions
If the subject is a nature (like 'the animal' meaning the essence), it may be treated as shakhsiyya, muhmala, or an independent division depending on the scholarly view.
For basic identification, you can treat it as muhmala unless the context indicates otherwise.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالَّذِي يَتَحَصَّلُ مِنْ كَلَامِ المُصَنِّفِ فِي هَذَا المَقَامِ : أَنَّ الحَمْلِيَّةَ أَرْبَعَةُ أَقْسَامٍ : (١) - الأَوَّلُ : الشَّخْصِيَّةُ ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا مُشَخَّصاً مُعَيَّناً ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : زَيْدٌ قَائِمٌ»، لَكِنْ يَمْتَنِعُ إِطْلَاقُ الشَّخْصِيَّةِ عَلَى نَحْوِ قَوْلِنَا : «اللَّهُ تَعَالَى قَادِرُه ؛ لِمَا فِيهِ مِنْ إِيْهَامٍ تَشَخُّصِ المَوْضُوعِ تَشَخُّصاً حِسْمَانِيًّا تَعَالَى اللَّهُ عَنْهُ . (٢) - وَالثَّانِي : المُهْمَلَةُ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ مَوْضُوعُهَا كُلِّيًّا، وَأُهْمِلَتْ مِنَ السُّوْرِ»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : الإِنْسَانُ حَيَوَانُ إِذَا جُعِلَتْ أَلْ لِلْجِنْسِ فِي ضِمْنِ الأَفْرَادِ بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الكُلِّيَةِ وَالجُزْيَيَّةِ. (٣) - وَالثَّالِثُ : الكُلِّيَّةُ، وَهِيَ : المُسَوَّرَةُ بِالسُّوْرِ الكُلّيّ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ . (٤) - وَالرَّابِعُ : الجُزْيَّةُ ، وَهِيَ : المُسَوَّرَةُ بِالسُّوْرِ الجُزْنِيّ؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : بَعْضُ الحَيَوَانِ إِنْسَانٌ . وَهَذَانِ القِسْمَانِ وَإِنْ لَمْ يُصَرِّحُ بِهِمَا المُصَنِّفُ، لَكِنَّهُمَا مَأخوذَانِ مِنْ قَوْلِهِ : وَالسُّوْرُ كُلِّيّا وَجُزئيا يُرَى، فَإِنَّهُ يُؤْخَذُ مِنْهُ : أَنَّ المُسَوَّرَةَ بِالسُّورِ الكُلِّيِّ تُسَمَّى : كُلِّيَّةَ ، وَالمُسَوَّرَةَ بِالسُّورِ الجُزْنِيِّ تُسَمَّى : جُزثِيَّة». وَلَمْ يَتَعَرَّضِ المُصَنِّفُ لِـ : الطَّبِيعِيَّةِ ، وَهِيَ : مَا كَانَ المَحْكُومُ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا الطَّبِيعَةُ، بِقَطْعِ النَّظَرِ عَنِ الأَفْرَادِ»؛ كَقَوْلِكَ : «الحَيَوَانُ جِنْسٌ»، وَقَدْ جَرَى فِيهَا خِلَافٌ: - فَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ الحَقُّ - : أَنَّهَا دَاخِلَةٌ فِي الشَّخْصِيَّةِ ؛ لأَنَّ المَحْكُومَ عَلَيْهِ فِيهَا مُشَخَّصُ ذِهْناً . - وَقِيلَ : إِنَّهَا دَاخِلَةٌ فِى المُهْمَلَةِ . - وَقِيلَ - وَهُوَ المَشْهُورُ - : إِنَّهَا قِسْمٌ مُسْتَقِل، لَا شَخْصِيَّةٌ وَلَا مُهْمَلَةٌ .
And what is obtained from the author's speech in this place is that the categorical [proposition] has four divisions: (1) First: the individual, which is that whose subject is specified individually; like your saying: 'Zayd is standing.' But it is forbidden to apply the term 'individual' to something like our saying: 'Allah Almighty is powerful'; because it contains an implication of the subject being specified in a physical way—Allah is exalted above that. (2) Second: the indefinite, which is that whose subject is universal and is left unspecified regarding the quantifier; like your saying: 'The human is an animal' when the 'al' is for the genus within individuals, regardless of universality and particularity. (3) Third: the universal, which is that which is quantified by the universal quantifier; like your saying: 'Every human is an animal.' (4) Fourth: the particular, which is that which is quantified by the particular quantifier; like your saying: 'Some animal is a human.' And these two divisions, even if the author did not explicitly mention them, they are taken from his saying: 'And the quantifier is seen as universal and particular.' For it is understood from it that what is quantified by the universal quantifier is called universal, and what is quantified by the particular quantifier is called particular. And the author did not address the natural [proposition], which is that whose subject is the nature, regardless of individuals; like your saying: 'The animal is a genus.' And there is a disagreement concerning it: it was said—and it is the truth—that it is included in the individual, because its subject is specified mentally. And it was said that it is included in the indefinite. And it was said—and it is the well-known [view]—that it is an independent division, neither individual nor indefinite.
Source pages p. 238

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of the matn's classification of categorical propositions. The commentator (al-Bajuri) clarifies that the categorical proposition has four divisions. The first is the individual (shakhsiyya), whose subject is a specific individual (e.g., 'Zayd is standing'). However, he cautions that one should not apply this term to God, as it would imply physical individuality. The second is the indefinite (muhmala), where the subject is universal but has no quantifier, such as 'The human is an animal' when the 'al' indicates the genus as it exists in individuals, without reference to all or some. The third is the universal (kulliyya), which has a universal quantifier like 'every' (e.g., 'Every human is an animal'). The fourth is the particular (juz'iyya), with a particular quantifier such as 'some' (e.g., 'Some animal is a human'). The commentator notes that the author (al-Akhdari) did not explicitly list the last two, but they are derived from his mention of universal and particular quantifiers ('And the quantifier is seen as universal and particular'). Finally, the commentator discusses the 'natural proposition' (tabi'iyya), where the subject is the abstract nature, e.g., 'The animal is a genus' (referring to the essence of animalness). The logicians differ on its classification: some include it in the individual because mentally it is a specific concept; others in the indefinite; and the well-known view treats it as an independent division. This classification is foundational for understanding how propositions are quantified in logic.

Key Terms
القضية الحمليّة (al-qadiyya al-hamliyya): A proposition whose subject and predicate are both single terms (or their equivalents), and it asserts or denies the predicate of the subject, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'. [Note: In some texts, 'hamliyya' is contrasted with 'shartiyya' (conditional); here it is the basic categorical proposition.]
الشَّخْصِيَّة (al-shakhsiyya): A proposition whose subject is a specific individual, e.g., 'Zayd is standing'; also called 'individual proposition'. [Note: In later logical works, sometimes called 'al-mahsusa' or 'al-‘ayniyya'; stable in meaning.]
المُهْمَلَة (al-muhmala): A proposition whose subject is a universal term without any quantifier, e.g., 'The human is an animal' (not specifying all or some). [Note: Stable across the tradition; also called 'al-mursala'.]
الكُلِّيَّة (al-kulliyya): A proposition quantified by a universal quantifier such as 'every' or 'none', e.g., 'Every human is an animal'. [Note: Distinct from 'kulli' as a term; here it is a type of proposition.]
الجُزْئِيَّة (al-juz'iyya): A proposition quantified by a particular quantifier such as 'some' or 'not all', e.g., 'Some animal is a human'. [Note: Stable.]
الطَّبِيعِيَّة (al-tabi'iyya): A proposition whose subject is the nature (i.e., the essence) without considering its individuals, e.g., 'The animal is a genus'. [Note: Its classification varies; some treat it as a separate type, others include it under individual or indefinite.]

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is an example of a 'shakhsiyya' (individual) proposition?
A) Every human is rational.
B) Some humans are writers.
C) Zayd is standing.
D) The human is an animal (indefinite).

Key Takeaway
Categorical propositions are classified into individual, indefinite, universal, and particular based on the specification of the subject and the presence of a quantifier.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Classify the following propositions: (1) 'Every intoxicant is haram.' (2) 'Zayd is praying.' (3) 'Some animals are rational.' (4) 'The human is mortal.' (indefinite)
?
Find a kulliyya proposition in a fiqh text (e.g., 'Every sale is valid unless prohibited') and explain why it is universal.

The Natural Proposition and Its Classification
Commentator discusses the natural proposition and the scholarly disagreement on its classification.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a natural proposition (tabi'iyya) and explain the three views on its classification among logicians.

Core Idea
The natural proposition exposes a subtle debate about whether abstract essences are mentally specified or not.

In addition to the four standard types, logicians discuss the 'natural proposition' (al-tabi'iyya), where the subject is the abstract nature or essence, e.g., 'The animal is a genus.' Al-Bajuri presents three opinions on how to classify it. This lesson unpacks that debate.

Guided Path
1
Define the natural proposition
It is a proposition where the subject is the nature (essence) considered in itself, without regard to its individuals. Example: 'The animal is a genus.'
The subject here is 'animal' in the sense of the essence of animalness, not any particular animal or all animals.
2
Understand the first view
Some say it is a type of individual (shakhsiyya) because the nature is mentally specified; in the mind, it is a specific concept.
This is considered the correct view by al-Bajuri.
3
Understand the second view
Others say it is indefinite (muhmala) because the term 'the animal' lacks a quantifier and could refer to the genus as a whole.
4
Understand the third view
The well-known view treats it as an independent division, distinct from both individual and indefinite.
This view holds that the natural proposition has unique logical properties.
5
Evaluate the implications
Consider how this classification affects logical operations like conversion or syllogistic validity when dealing with propositions about essences.
For instance, if it is individual, it cannot be used as a universal premise; if indefinite, it is treated as particular in syllogisms.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

Three Permanent Propositions: Absolute, General Customary, Specific Customary
Author defines the three types of permanent propositions with examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify and distinguish the three types of permanent propositions (al-dawa'im al-thalath) by their definitions and examples.

Core Idea
Modal permanence in propositions is conditioned by attributes — or not.

In this passage, the author presents the three permanent propositions that form the bedrock of modal logic in the Islamic tradition. By mastering their definitions and examples, you will be able to correctly classify any modal proposition encountered in theological or juristic reasoning. We will walk through each type, its condition, and how the specific customary is composed from simpler components.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories
Read the Arabic and list the three types: al-da'imah al-mutlaqah, al-'urfiyyah al-'ammah, al-'urfiyyah al-khassah.
These are the three 'permanent' propositions.
2
Extract the defining condition
For each type, identify what condition (if any) qualifies the permanence of the relation. The absolute permanent has no condition; the general customary requires the subject's attribute to persist; the specific customary adds that it is not always true beyond the attribute.
The condition is key to distinguishing them.
3
Match examples to types
Given the examples in the passage (e.g., 'Every human is an animal always'), identify which type each exemplifies.
Pay attention to the presence or absence of 'as long as' and 'not always'.
4
Analyze composition of the specific customary
The specific customary is composite. Break down the affirmative example into a general customary affirmative and a general absolute negative.
This shows how modal propositions are built from simpler ones.

Source Text

وَالدَّوَائِمُ الثَّلَاثُ، وَهِيَ :
(۸) - الدَّائِمَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ وَهِيَ : «الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ مَعَ الإِطْلَاقِ عَنِ التَّفْسِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ» ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانٍ حَيَوَانٌ دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الْإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ دَائِماً». وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: «دَائِمَةٌ» ؛ لِأَنَّ كَيْفِيَّةَ نِسْبَتِهَا الدَّوَامُ، وَ : «مُطْلَقَةٌ»؛ لِإِطْلَاقِهَا عَنِ التَّفْيِيدِ بِوَصْفٍ أَوْ نَحْوِهِ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَمَا يُعْلَمُ مِمَّا يَأْتِي.
(۹) - وَالعُرْفِيَّةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : «الَّتي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ النِّسْبَةِ بِشَرْطِ دَوَامِ وَصْفِ المَوْضُوعِ»؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، وَسَالِبَةٌ: لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً ما دَامَ كَاتِباً .
وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ : «عُرْفِيَّةٌ» ؛ لِانْفِهَامِ التَّقْيِيدِ فِيهَا بِدَوَامِ الوَصْفِ مِنَ العُرْفِ، وَلَوْ لَمْ يُصَرَّحْ بِهِ و: «عَامَّةٌ»؛ لِأَنَّهَا أَعَمُّ مِنَ العُرْفِيَّةِ الخَاصَّةِ، فَإِنَّهَا لَمْ تُقَيَّدْ بِمَا يَنْفِي احْتِمَالَ الدَّوَامِ وَهُوَ قَوْلُنَا : لَا دَائِماً ، كَمَا تَقَدَّمَ نَظِيرُهُ ، وَهِيَ بَسِيطَةٌ كَالَّتِي قَبْلَهَا .
(١٠) - وَالعُرْفِيَّةُ الخَاصَّةُ وَهِيَ : «العُرْفِيَّةُ العَامَّةُ لَكِنْ مَعَ زِيَادَةِ قَيْدِ : لَا دَائِماً » ؛ مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّلُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً»، وَسَالِبَةٌ لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً لَا دَائِماً».
وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَتْ: «عُرْفِيَّةٌ» ; لِمَا مَرَّ، وَ : «خَاصَّةٌ » ; لِأَنَّهَا أَخَصُّ مِنَ العُرْفِيَّةِ العَامَّةِ كَمَا عُلِمَ مِمَّا مَرَّ، وَهِيَ مُرَكَّبَةٌ :
- إِنْ كَانَتْ مُوجَبَةٌ : مِنْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : «كُلُّ كَاتِبٍ مُتَحَرِّكُ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْزُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»; لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ: «لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِمُتَحَرِّكِ الأصابع بالإطلاقِ العام»; لِمَا مَرَّ .
- وَإِنْ كَانَتْ سَالِبَةٌ : مِنْ عُرْفِيَّةٍ عَامَّةٍ سَالِبَةٍ وَهِيَ الصَّدْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ مَثَلاً : «لَا شَيْءَ مِنَ الكَاتِبِ بِسَاكِنِ الأَصَابِعِ دَائِماً مَا دَامَ كَاتِباً، فَمُطْلَقَةٍ عَامَّةٍ مُوجَبَةٍ وَهِيَ العَجْرُ ; أَعْنِي قَوْلَكَ : «لَا دَائِماً»; لِأَنَّهُ فِي قُوَّةِ أَنْ يُقَالَ : كُلُّ كَاتِبِ سَاكِنُ الأَصَابِعِ بِالإِطْلَاقِ العَامُ؛ لِمَا مَرَّ .
And the three permanent (propositions) are: (8) The absolute permanent: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged with absoluteness from restriction by an attribute or the like. Its example as affirmative: 'Every human is an animal always', and as negative: 'No human is a stone always'. It was named 'permanent' because the quality of its relation is permanence, and 'absolute' because of its freedom from restriction by an attribute or the like. It is simple as will be known from what follows. (9) The general customary: it is that in which the permanence of the relation is judged on condition of the permanence of the subject's attribute. Its example as affirmative: 'Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer', and as negative: 'No writer is still-fingered always as long as he is a writer'. It was named 'customary' because the restriction to the permanence of the attribute is understood from custom even if not explicitly stated, and 'general' because it is more general than the specific customary, since it is not restricted by what negates the possibility of permanence, which is our saying: 'not always', as its counterpart preceded. It is simple like the one before. (10) The specific customary: it is the general customary but with the addition of the restriction 'not always'. Its example as affirmative: 'Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer, not always', and as negative: 'No writer is still-fingered always as long as he is a writer, not always'. It was named 'customary' for what preceded, and 'specific' because it is more specific than the general customary as known from what preceded. It is composite: - If it is affirmative: (it is composed) of a general customary affirmative which is the beginning, i.e., your saying for example: 'Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer', and a general absolute negative which is the end, i.e., your saying: 'not always', because it has the force of 'No writer is moving fingers with general absoluteness', for what preceded. - And if it is negative: (it is composed) of a general customary negative which is the beginning, i.e., your saying for example: 'No writer is still-fingered always as long as he is a writer', and a general absolute affirmative which is the end, i.e., your saying: 'not always', because it has the force of 'Every writer is still-fingered with general absoluteness', for what preceded.
Source pages p. 253

Commentary
This passage is a definitional enumeration of the three types of permanent propositions (al-dawa'im al-thalath) in modal logic. The author defines each type, gives examples (both affirmative and negative), explains the etymology of their names, and comments on their complexity. The three types are: (1) al-da'imah al-mutlaqah (the absolute permanent), where the relation holds always without any condition; e.g., 'Every human is an animal always' and 'No human is a stone always'. (2) al-'urfiyyah al-'ammah (the general customary), where the relation holds always as long as a certain attribute of the subject persists; e.g., 'Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer'. (3) al-'urfiyyah al-khassah (the specific customary), which is the general customary but with the negation of permanence (i.e., 'not always') added, indicating that the relation does not hold beyond the duration of the attribute. The author then analyzes the composition of the specific customary: an affirmative example combines a general customary affirmative with a general absolute negative, while a negative example combines a general customary negative with a general absolute affirmative. This shows how modal propositions are built from simpler components.

Key Terms
الدَّائِمَةُ المُطْلَقَةُ
العُرْفِيَّةُ العَامَّةُ
العُرْفِيَّةُ الخَاصَّةُ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following examples best fits the definition of 'al-'urfiyyah al-'ammah'?
A) Every human is an animal always.
B) Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer.
C) Every writer is moving fingers always as long as he is a writer, not always.
D) No human is a stone always.

Key Takeaway
The three permanent propositions differ solely in the condition attached to the permanence: absolute (no condition), general customary (attribute-based condition), and specific customary (attribute-based but with negation of absolute permanence).

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Take the proposition 'Every human is rational always as long as they are human'. Identify which type of permanent proposition it is, and explain why.
?
Construct an example of a specific customary proposition using a fiqh ruling (e.g., regarding a traveler's prayer).

Defining the General Possible Proposition
Author defines the general possible proposition and provides examples.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify a general possible proposition in a modal context and distinguish it from a specific possible proposition.

Core Idea
The general possible only negates the necessity of the opposite – it does not require the proposition itself to be contingent.

In modal logic, propositions are classified by modality. The general possible proposition is a broad category that includes even necessary truths, because it only requires that the opposite is not necessary. This lesson unpacks the definition and examples from the matn.

Guided Path
1
Understand the definition
The passage defines the general possible proposition as 'that in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the opposite side.' Identify what 'opposite side' refers to: the contradictory or contrary of the proposition.
The opposite side is the negation of the proposition.
2
Examine the affirmative example
The example 'Every human is an animal with general possibility' – even though this proposition is necessary (a human cannot be other than an animal), it is considered possible here because its opposite (some human is not an animal) is not necessary.
It is not necessary that some human is not an animal.
3
Examine the negative example
The negative example 'No human is a stone with general possibility' – similarly, the opposite (some human is a stone) is not necessary; in fact, it is impossible, but the general possible only negates its necessity.
The impossible opposite is not necessary, so it qualifies.
4
Contrast with specific possible
The specific possible proposition (al-mumkinah al-khassah) additionally negates necessity from the subject-predicate relation itself. The general possible is broader and includes necessary propositions.
The specific possible excludes necessary propositions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وَالمُمْكِنَتَانِ، وَهُمَا :
(۱۱) - المُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ وَهِيَ : الَّتِي حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِسَلْبِ الضَّرُورَةِ عَنِ الطَّرَفِ المُخَالِفِ» ; مِثَالُهَا مُوجَبَةٌ : كُلُّ إِنْسَانِ حَيَوَانُ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَامٌ ، وَسَالِبَةٌ : لَا شَيْءٍ مِنَ الإِنْسَانِ بِحَجَرٍ بِالإِمْكَانِ العَام .
And the two possible (propositions) are: (11) The general possible: it is that in which it is judged by the negation of necessity from the opposite side. Its example as affirmative: 'Every human is an animal with general possibility', and as negative: 'No human is a stone with general possibility'.
Source pages p. 254

Commentary
This passage defines the general possible proposition (al-mumkinah al-'ammah), which is one of the two types of possible propositions. The definition focuses on the negation of necessity: the proposition asserts that the opposite relation is not necessary. For an affirmative example, 'Every human is an animal', the opposite is 'Some human is not an animal' (or 'No human is an animal'?), but the definition says 'negation of necessity from the opposite side' meaning that the contradictory or contrary is not necessary. In the example, 'Every human is an animal with general possibility' means that it is not necessary that some human is not an animal (i.e., the possibility of the proposition is that it could be otherwise). Similarly, the negative example 'No human is a stone with general possibility' means it is not necessary that some human is a stone. This definition is broader than the specific possible proposition, which additionally negates necessity from the affirmative side as well (i.e., the relation is truly contingent). Here, the general possible only requires that the opposite is not necessary, so it includes propositions that are actually necessary (like 'Every human is an animal' is necessary but also possible in this sense).

Key Terms
المُمْكِنَةُ العَامَّةُ
سَلْبُ الضَّرُورَةِ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the definition, which of the following would be considered a general possible proposition?
A) Every human is an animal by necessity (necessary proposition).
B) Every human is an animal with general possibility.
C) Every human is a writer by possibility (contingent).
D) No human is a stone by necessity.

Key Takeaway
The general possible proposition is defined solely by the negation of the necessity of the opposite, making it a broader category that includes necessary truths.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Identify which of the following propositions would be considered a general possible proposition: 'Every created thing is temporally originated by necessity' (necessary), 'Some humans are scholars by contingency' (contingent). Explain your reasoning.
?
Take a necessary proposition from Kalam (e.g., 'The world is temporally originated' if accepted as necessary) and show that it also qualifies as a general possible proposition per the definition.

Composite and Simple Modal Propositions
Author defines composite and simple propositions within the fifteen modal system.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify any given modal proposition as composite (murakkaba) or simple (basita) using the presence of specific modal qualifiers.

Core Idea
A proposition's composite or simple status hinges on a single modal term: 'not always', 'not necessarily', or 'specific possibility'.

In the study of modal logic, the fifteen propositions form a core set, but not all are equal. Some are composite, containing a modal qualifier, while others are simple. This distinction is crucial for understanding how modalities interact in Islamic theological and legal reasoning. In this lesson, we will unpack the author's definition and apply it to real examples from Kalam and Usul al-Fiqh.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Locate the terms 'murakkaba' (composite) and 'basita' (simple) in the Arabic passage. Understand that these are subcategories of the fifteen modal propositions.
The passage states: 'they divide into composite and simple'.
2
Extract the defining criteria for composite
The composite proposition is defined by containing any of these three modal qualifiers: 'not always' (lā dā'iman), 'not necessarily' (lā ḍarūratan), or 'specific possibility' (al-imkān al-khāṣṣ). List them.
The author explicitly names these qualifiers.
3
Understand the simple by exclusion
The simple proposition is defined as everything else—any modal proposition that lacks all three qualifiers. This is a negative definition.
The passage says: 'And the simple is other than that'.
4
Test with examples from Islamic sciences
Apply the criteria to sample propositions. For instance, 'Allah is necessarily existing' contains no qualifier → simple. 'The world is not always temporally originated' contains 'not always' → composite.
Use real examples from Kalam (e.g., possibility of creation) or Fiqh (e.g., rulings with modalities).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فَهَذِهِ المَذْكُورَاتُ جُمْلَةُ الخَمْسَةَ عَشَرَ ، وَبَعْضُهُمْ نَقَصَ عَنْهَا وَبَعْضُهُمْ زَادَ عَلَيْهَا، حَتَّى قَالَ بَعْضُهُمْ: إِنَّهَا لَا تَنْحَصِرُ فِي عَدَدٍ. وَعُلِمَ مِمَّا تَقَرَّرَ : أَنَّهَا تَنْقَسِمُ إِلَى مُرَكَّبَةٍ وَبَسِيطَةٍ، فَالمُرَكَّبَةُ : مَا كَانَ فِيهَا زِيَادَةُ لا دَائِماً أَوْ لا ضَرُورَة، أَوْ كَانَ فِيهَا الإِمْكَانُ الخَاصُ وَالبَسِيطَهُ مَا عَدَا ذَلِكَ، وَقَدْ أَشَارَ بَعْضُهُمْ لِذَلِكَ بِقَوْلِهِ : [من الرجز] وَمَا حَوَى مِنَ القَضَايَا لَا ، كَذَا أَوْ خَاصٌ إِمْكَانٍ مُرَكَباً خُذَا وَمَا خَلَا عَنْ ذَيْنِ فَالبَسِيط فَادْعُ لِمَنْ أَلَّفَ يَا نَـ وَالكَلَامُ عَلَى المُوَجَّهَاتِ كَثِيرٌ ، وَقَدْ أُفْرِدَتْ بِالتَّأْلِيفِ، وَفِي هَذَا القَدْرِ كِفَايَةٌ.
These mentioned propositions are the total of fifteen. Some reduced from them and some added to them, until some said: they are not limited to a number. And it is known from what has been established: that they divide into composite and simple. The composite is that in which there is an addition of 'not always' or 'not necessarily', or in which there is the specific possibility. And the simple is other than that. And some have indicated that by his saying: [from rajaz] 'What contains from the propositions 'not' or 'specific possibility', take it as composite, and what is free from these two, then call it simple, for he who composed, O ...' And the discussion on modalities is extensive, and they have been treated in specialized works, and in this amount is sufficient.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a definitional explanation of the division of propositions into composite and simple. The author first states that the fifteen modal propositions are not fixed in number, as some scholars add or subtract. Then he defines the composite proposition as one that contains a modal term such as 'not always' (la da'iman), 'not necessarily' (la daruratan), or 'specific possibility' (al-imkan al-khas). The simple proposition is everything else. He supports this with a poetic couplet from a previous scholar (likely al-Tilimsani) that instructs: treat propositions containing 'not' or 'specific possibility' as composite, and others as simple. The passage concludes by noting that the topic of modalities is vast and has been treated in dedicated works.

Key Terms
مُرَكَّبَة
بَسِيطَة
الخَمْسَةَ عَشَرَ

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to this passage, what makes a proposition 'composite' (murakkaba)?
A) It contains a conditional connector.
B) It has a modal term like 'not always' or 'specific possibility'.
C) It has more than one subject.
D) It is a hypothetical syllogism.

Key Takeaway
A modal proposition is composite if it includes 'not always', 'not necessarily', or 'specific possibility'; otherwise it is simple.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Subject and Predicate: Logical Rank and Definition
Definition of subject and predicate with rank explanation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify the subject (mawdu') and predicate (mahmul) in any categorical proposition and explain their logical rank based on the substance-attribute relationship.

Core Idea
The subject is first in rank as the substance bearing the predicate, even if mentioned last.

This lesson unpacks the definitions of subject and predicate in categorical propositions. The passage explains why the subject is considered 'first in rank' despite sometimes appearing after the predicate, drawing on the analogy of substance and attribute. You will learn to apply these logical roles to analyze propositions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the subject and predicate in the passage
Locate where the author defines 'mawdu'' and 'mahmul'. Notice that the subject is described as 'the first in rank' and the predicate as 'the last in rank' regardless of word order.
2
Understand the reason for rank
The subject is that about which something is judged (the described), and the predicate is the description. In meaning, the described precedes its description, hence the subject is logically prior.
3
Compare with Arabic grammar
The text compares with mubtada' and khabar (precedence of mubtada') and fa'il (which follows verb due to syntactic operation). Note how logical rank may differ from linguistic order.
The fa'il is a described entity but delayed because the verb is the 'āmil (operator).
4
Apply the substance-attribute principle
As al-Anbabi explains, the subject in origin is a substance (dhāt) and the predicate is an attribute (waṣf). The substance is more entitled to be the bearer, so it is logically first.

قَوْلُهُ : (المَوضُوعُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ)) . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ وُضِعَ لِيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ؛ كَمَا قَالَهُ ابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ. وَالمُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ : الأَوَّلُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ آخِراً، كَمَا أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بـ «المَحْمُولِ»: الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ المَوضُوعُ أَوَّلاً فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَالمَحْمُولُ آخِراً فِيهَا ؛ لأنَّ المَوْضُوعَ مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهِ بِالمَحْمُولِ، وَالمَحْكُومُ بِهِ وَصْفٌ لِلمَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي المَعْنَى ، وَالمَوْصُوفُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى صِفَتِهِ، وَلِهَذَا جَعَلَ النُّحاةُ رُتْبَةَ المُبْتَدَأَ التَّقَدُّمُ، وَرُتْبَةَ الخَبَرِ التَّأْخُرُ ، وَإِنَّمَا جَعَلُوا رُتْبَةَ الفَاعِلِ التَّأْخُرُ عَنِ الفِعْلِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ مَوْصُوفٌ فِي المَعْنَى لِأَمْرٍ لَفْظِيٍّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الفِعْلَ عَامِلٌ فِيهِ، وَرُتُبةُ العَامِلِ التَّقَدُّمُ عَلَى مَعْمُولِهِ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ . الأنبابي قوله : لِأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ... إلخ) سببُه : أنَّ الموضوع أصله أن يكون ذاتاً، والمحمول أصله أن يكون وصفاً، والذَّاتُ أحقُّ بأن تكون حاملة، والوصفُ أحقُّ بأن يكون محمولاً [ج/ ٢٢]. ثم في صفحة 259: باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : (بِالحَمْلِيَّة) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِمَحْذُوفٍ صِفَةٌ لِـ «الأَوَّلِ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَالأَوَّلُ الكَائِنُ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ المَوْضُوعُ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالآخِرُ) - بِكَسْرِ الخَاءِ - بِمَعْنَى : المُتَأخّرِ، لَا بِفَتْحِهَا بِمَعْنَى : المُغَايرِ ؛ بِدَلِيلِ مُقَابَلَتِهِ بـ «الأَوَّلِ ، وَالمُرَادُ : الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً ، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (المَحْمُولُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الآخِر»، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ حُمِلَ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ عِبَارَةِ ابْنِ يَعْقُوبَ. قَوْلُهُ : (بِالسَّوِيَّة) أَيْ : حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا مُلْتَبِسَيْنِ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ بِمَعْنَى : الاسْتِوَاءِ فِي الذِّكْرِ؛ بِحَيْثُ لَا يُذْكَرُ أَحَدُهُمَا دُونَ الآخَرِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ (١) ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا حُكْماً كَايْناً عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعْلِيقِ(٢) لَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، فَإِنَّهَا إلخ، وَعَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ فَ عَلَى» بَاقِيَةٌ عَلَى بَابِهَا . وَيُحْتَمَلُ - وَهُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ وَتَبِعَهُ غَيْرُهُ - أَنَّهَا بِمَعْنَى : «البَاءِ»؛ وَالمَعْنَى : وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّعْلِيقِ، فَإِنَّهَا ... إلخ (٣). فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ تَوْقِيفُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَهَذَا خَاصٌ بِالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيَقْسُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «التَّعْلِيقِ فِي كَلَامِهِ : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ، الأنبابي قوله : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ) أي : سواء كان على وجه التَّوقف والتَّرتُب، أو على وجه التّنافي، والمراد : ربط مخصوص يشمل القسمين، لا مطلق ربط يشملهما وغيرهما، وإلا لم يحصل التمييز عن الحملية، ولم يكن فائدةً لقوله : عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ».
His saying: 'The subject is a predicate about the first.' And it is only called that because it is imagined to be like something placed for something else to be predicated on it, as Ibn Ya'qub said. And what is named by that is the first in rank even if mentioned last, just as what is named 'predicate' is the last in rank even if mentioned first. And the subject is only first in rank and the predicate last because the subject is that about which something is judged by the predicate, and that which is judged by (the predicate) is a description of the subject in meaning, and the described is prior to its description. For this reason, the grammarians made the rank of the subject (mubtada') precedence and the rank of the predicate (khabar) delay. And they made the rank of the agent (fa'il) delay after the verb, even though it is described in meaning, due to a verbal matter, which is that the verb is an 'amil (operator) on it, and the rank of the operator is precedence over its operand. So let one ponder. al-Anbabi: His saying: 'because it is imagined... etc.' Its reason is that the subject in origin is a substance, and the predicate in origin is an attribute, and the substance is more entitled to be the bearer, and the attribute is more entitled to be carried. Then on page 259: Chapter on propositions and their rulings. (56) 'And the first is the subject in the categorical, and the last is the predicate equally. (57) And if in it there is a judgment on the basis of connection, then it is a conditional, and it divides.' al-Bajuri: His saying: 'in the categorical' is attached to a deleted word that is a description of 'the first', and the estimation is: 'and the first that is in the categorical is the subject.' His saying: 'and the last' – with kasra on the kha' – meaning the later, not with fatha meaning the different; as evidence of its contrast with 'the first'. And what is meant is the last in rank even if mentioned first, as you know. And his saying: 'the predicate' is a predicate about 'the last'. And it is only called that because it is imagined to be like something carried on something else, as taken from Ibn Ya'qub's expression. His saying: 'equally' meaning, while both are equally adorned, meaning equality in mention, so that neither is mentioned without the other. His saying: 'And if on the basis of connection... etc.' meaning: and if in it there is a judgment that is on the basis of connection, not on the basis of predication, then it is... etc. And on this estimation, 'on' remains in its original sense. And it is possible – and this is what al-Malawi restricted himself to, and others followed him – that it means 'with' (ba'), and the meaning: and if it is judged with connection, then it is... etc. For if it is said: It is not hidden that connection is the suspending of one thing on another, and this is specific to the conditional conjunctive, even though the author will divide the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive. It is answered: That what is meant by 'connection' in his statement is the linking between the two parts, even if on the basis of opposition. al-Anbabi: His saying: 'the linking between the two parts even if on the basis of opposition' meaning: whether on the basis of dependence and sequence, or on the basis of conflict. And what is meant is a specific connection that includes both types, not an absolute connection that includes them and others; otherwise the distinction from categorical would not be obtained, and there would be no benefit in his saying 'on the basis of connection'.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text. It begins by explaining why the subject is called 'subject' (mawdu'): it is imagined as something placed for another thing to be predicated on it. The subject is first in rank even if mentioned last, and the predicate is last in rank even if mentioned first, because the subject is the thing being described and the predicate is the description. The commentator draws an analogy with Arabic grammar: the mubtada' comes before the khabar, but the fa'il comes after the verb due to a syntactic rule. Then al-Anbabi adds that the subject is essentially a substance (dhat) and the predicate an attribute (wasf), and the substance is more fitting to bear the attribute. On page 259, the matn defines the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition, and then introduces conditional propositions: if the judgment involves a link (ta'liq), then the proposition is conditional (shartiyya). Al-Bajuri explains that 'in the categorical' describes 'the first', and 'the last' means the later in rank. 'Equally' means both are mentioned together. He then clarifies the meaning of 'on the basis of connection': either 'on' retains its sense, or it means 'with' (ba'). An objection is raised: ta'liq implies dependence, which is specific to conjunctive conditionals, yet the author later divides conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive. The answer is that ta'liq here means a link between the two parts, whether by dependence or by opposition, thus covering both types. Al-Anbabi reinforces that this link is a specific binding that distinguishes conditional from categorical.

Checkpoint
Why is the subject considered 'first in rank' even if it appears last in a sentence?
A) Because grammarians require the subject to come first.
B) Because the subject is the substance that bears the attribute, and the described is prior to its description.
C) Because the predicate is always mentioned first.
D) Because the subject is always mentioned first in the Arabic language.

Key Takeaway
The subject of a categorical proposition is logically prior as the substance bearing the predicate, regardless of word order.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Analyze the categorical proposition 'Khamr is haram.' Identify the subject and predicate, and explain why the subject is first in rank even though 'khamr' appears first.
?
In the definition of 'salat' as 'specific words and actions beginning with takbir and ending with taslim', identify the subject and predicate. Is the subject first in rank? Justify.

Conditional Propositions: Definition and the Scope of Ta'liq
Definition of conditional proposition and explanation of ta'liq

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish a conditional proposition from a categorical one by recognizing the presence of ta'liq (connection) and explain why ta'liq encompasses both conjunctive and disjunctive conditionals.

Core Idea
A conditional proposition is defined by a link (ta'liq) between its parts, covering dependence or opposition.

This lesson focuses on the definition of conditional propositions (qadiyya shartiyya). The text explains that if the judgment is made on the basis of a connection (ta'liq), the proposition is conditional. A potential objection arises because ta'liq seems to imply dependence, which fits only conjunctive conditionals. The resolution shows that ta'liq here means a broader link that includes both conjunctive and disjunctive types. You will learn to identify conditional propositions and understand the nuanced meaning of ta'liq.

Guided Path
1
Identify the defining phrase for conditionals
Find where the author says: 'If in it there is a judgment on the basis of connection, then it is a conditional.' Note the contrast with categorical propositions.
2
Understand the two possible interpretations of 'on'
Al-Bajuri explains that 'on' (على) can retain its literal meaning or be interpreted as 'with' (باء). Either way, the judgment is made through a connection.
3
Consider the objection and its answer
The objection says ta'liq (connection) implies dependence, which is only for conjunctive conditionals. The answer broadens ta'liq to mean any linking, even by opposition (عِناد), thus covering disjunctive.
Al-Anbabi reinforces that this link is specific to conditionals and not any arbitrary link.
4
Apply the definition to examples
Given a proposition, determine whether it is conditional by checking if there is a connection between two clauses, either dependent or mutually exclusive.

قَوْلُهُ : (المَوضُوعُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الأَوَّلِ)) . وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لِأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ وُضِعَ لِيُحْمَلَ عَلَيْهِ غَيْرُهُ؛ كَمَا قَالَهُ ابْنُ يَعْقُوبَ. وَالمُسَمَّى بِذَلِكَ : الأَوَّلُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ آخِراً، كَمَا أَنَّ المُسَمَّى بـ «المَحْمُولِ»: الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً . وَإِنَّمَا كَانَ المَوضُوعُ أَوَّلاً فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَالمَحْمُولُ آخِراً فِيهَا ؛ لأنَّ المَوْضُوعَ مَحْكُومٌ عَلَيْهِ بِالمَحْمُولِ، وَالمَحْكُومُ بِهِ وَصْفٌ لِلمَحْكُوم عَلَيْهِ فِي المَعْنَى ، وَالمَوْصُوفُ سَابِقٌ عَلَى صِفَتِهِ، وَلِهَذَا جَعَلَ النُّحاةُ رُتْبَةَ المُبْتَدَأَ التَّقَدُّمُ، وَرُتْبَةَ الخَبَرِ التَّأْخُرُ ، وَإِنَّمَا جَعَلُوا رُتْبَةَ الفَاعِلِ التَّأْخُرُ عَنِ الفِعْلِ مَعَ أَنَّهُ مَوْصُوفٌ فِي المَعْنَى لِأَمْرٍ لَفْظِيٍّ، وَهُوَ أَنَّ الفِعْلَ عَامِلٌ فِيهِ، وَرُتُبةُ العَامِلِ التَّقَدُّمُ عَلَى مَعْمُولِهِ، فَلْيُتَامَّلْ . الأنبابي قوله : لِأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ... إلخ) سببُه : أنَّ الموضوع أصله أن يكون ذاتاً، والمحمول أصله أن يكون وصفاً، والذَّاتُ أحقُّ بأن تكون حاملة، والوصفُ أحقُّ بأن يكون محمولاً [ج/ ٢٢]. ثم في صفحة 259: باب في القضايا وأحكامها (٥٦) وَالأَوَّلُ: «المَوضُوعُ بِالحَمْلِيَّة وَالآخِرُ المَحْمُولُ بِالسَّوِيَّة (٥٧) وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ فِيهَا قَدْ حُكِمْ فَإِنَّهَا: «شَرْطِيَّةٌ»، وَتَنْقَسِمْ الباجوري قَوْلُهُ : (بِالحَمْلِيَّة) مُتَعَلِّقٌ بِمَحْذُوفٍ صِفَةٌ لِـ «الأَوَّلِ، وَالتَّقْدِيرُ : وَالأَوَّلُ الكَائِنُ فِي الحَمْلِيَّةِ المَوْضُوعُ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَالآخِرُ) - بِكَسْرِ الخَاءِ - بِمَعْنَى : المُتَأخّرِ، لَا بِفَتْحِهَا بِمَعْنَى : المُغَايرِ ؛ بِدَلِيلِ مُقَابَلَتِهِ بـ «الأَوَّلِ ، وَالمُرَادُ : الآخِرُ فِي الرُّتْبَةِ وَإِنْ ذُكِرَ أَوَّلاً ، كَمَا عَلِمْتَ. وَقَوْلُهُ : (المَحْمُولُ خَبَرٌ عَنِ الآخِر»، وَإِنَّمَا سُمِّيَ بِذَلِكَ ؛ لأَنَّهُ يُتَخَيَّلُ أَنَّهُ كَشَيْءٍ حُمِلَ عَلَى غَيْرِهِ، كَمَا يُؤْخَذُ مِنْ عِبَارَةِ ابْنِ يَعْقُوبَ. قَوْلُهُ : (بِالسَّوِيَّة) أَيْ : حَالَ كَوْنِهِمَا مُلْتَبِسَيْنِ بِالسَّوِيَّةِ بِمَعْنَى : الاسْتِوَاءِ فِي الذِّكْرِ؛ بِحَيْثُ لَا يُذْكَرُ أَحَدُهُمَا دُونَ الآخَرِ . قَوْلُهُ : (وَإِنْ عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ (١) ... إلخ) أَيْ: وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا حُكْماً كَايْناً عَلَى وَجْهِ التَّعْلِيقِ(٢) لَا عَلَى وَجْهِ الحَمْلِ، فَإِنَّهَا إلخ، وَعَلَى هَذَا التَّقْدِيرِ فَ عَلَى» بَاقِيَةٌ عَلَى بَابِهَا . وَيُحْتَمَلُ - وَهُوَ الَّذِي اقْتَصَرَ عَلَيْهِ الشَّيْخُ المَلوِيُّ وَتَبِعَهُ غَيْرُهُ - أَنَّهَا بِمَعْنَى : «البَاءِ»؛ وَالمَعْنَى : وَإِنْ حُكِمَ فِيهَا بِالتَّعْلِيقِ، فَإِنَّهَا ... إلخ (٣). فَإِنْ قِيلَ : لَا يَخْفَى أَنَّ التَّعْلِيقَ تَوْقِيفُ شَيْءٍ عَلَى شَيْءٍ آخَرَ، وَهَذَا خَاصٌ بِالشَّرْطِيَّةِ المُتَّصِلَةِ، مَعَ أَنَّ المُصَنِّفَ سَيَقْسُمُ الشَّرْطِيَّةَ : إِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُتَّصِلَةٍ، وَإِلَى شَرْطِيَّةٍ مُنْفَصِلَةٍ. أُجِيبَ : بِأَنَّ المُرَادَ بِـ «التَّعْلِيقِ فِي كَلَامِهِ : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ، الأنبابي قوله : الرَّبْطُ بَيْنَ الجُزْأَيْنِ وَلَوْ عَلَى وَجْهِ العِنَادِ) أي : سواء كان على وجه التَّوقف والتَّرتُب، أو على وجه التّنافي، والمراد : ربط مخصوص يشمل القسمين، لا مطلق ربط يشملهما وغيرهما، وإلا لم يحصل التمييز عن الحملية، ولم يكن فائدةً لقوله : عَلَى التَّعْلِيقِ».
His saying: 'The subject is a predicate about the first.' And it is only called that because it is imagined to be like something placed for something else to be predicated on it, as Ibn Ya'qub said. And what is named by that is the first in rank even if mentioned last, just as what is named 'predicate' is the last in rank even if mentioned first. And the subject is only first in rank and the predicate last because the subject is that about which something is judged by the predicate, and that which is judged by (the predicate) is a description of the subject in meaning, and the described is prior to its description. For this reason, the grammarians made the rank of the subject (mubtada') precedence and the rank of the predicate (khabar) delay. And they made the rank of the agent (fa'il) delay after the verb, even though it is described in meaning, due to a verbal matter, which is that the verb is an 'amil (operator) on it, and the rank of the operator is precedence over its operand. So let one ponder. al-Anbabi: His saying: 'because it is imagined... etc.' Its reason is that the subject in origin is a substance, and the predicate in origin is an attribute, and the substance is more entitled to be the bearer, and the attribute is more entitled to be carried. Then on page 259: Chapter on propositions and their rulings. (56) 'And the first is the subject in the categorical, and the last is the predicate equally. (57) And if in it there is a judgment on the basis of connection, then it is a conditional, and it divides.' al-Bajuri: His saying: 'in the categorical' is attached to a deleted word that is a description of 'the first', and the estimation is: 'and the first that is in the categorical is the subject.' His saying: 'and the last' – with kasra on the kha' – meaning the later, not with fatha meaning the different; as evidence of its contrast with 'the first'. And what is meant is the last in rank even if mentioned first, as you know. And his saying: 'the predicate' is a predicate about 'the last'. And it is only called that because it is imagined to be like something carried on something else, as taken from Ibn Ya'qub's expression. His saying: 'equally' meaning, while both are equally adorned, meaning equality in mention, so that neither is mentioned without the other. His saying: 'And if on the basis of connection... etc.' meaning: and if in it there is a judgment that is on the basis of connection, not on the basis of predication, then it is... etc. And on this estimation, 'on' remains in its original sense. And it is possible – and this is what al-Malawi restricted himself to, and others followed him – that it means 'with' (ba'), and the meaning: and if it is judged with connection, then it is... etc. For if it is said: It is not hidden that connection is the suspending of one thing on another, and this is specific to the conditional conjunctive, even though the author will divide the conditional into conjunctive and disjunctive. It is answered: That what is meant by 'connection' in his statement is the linking between the two parts, even if on the basis of opposition. al-Anbabi: His saying: 'the linking between the two parts even if on the basis of opposition' meaning: whether on the basis of dependence and sequence, or on the basis of conflict. And what is meant is a specific connection that includes both types, not an absolute connection that includes them and others; otherwise the distinction from categorical would not be obtained, and there would be no benefit in his saying 'on the basis of connection'.
Source pages p. 258

Commentary
This passage is a definitional and explanatory text. It begins by explaining why the subject is called 'subject' (mawdu'): it is imagined as something placed for another thing to be predicated on it. The subject is first in rank even if mentioned last, and the predicate is last in rank even if mentioned first, because the subject is the thing being described and the predicate is the description. The commentator draws an analogy with Arabic grammar: the mubtada' comes before the khabar, but the fa'il comes after the verb due to a syntactic rule. Then al-Anbabi adds that the subject is essentially a substance (dhat) and the predicate an attribute (wasf), and the substance is more fitting to bear the attribute. On page 259, the matn defines the subject and predicate in a categorical proposition, and then introduces conditional propositions: if the judgment involves a link (ta'liq), then the proposition is conditional (shartiyya). Al-Bajuri explains that 'in the categorical' describes 'the first', and 'the last' means the later in rank. 'Equally' means both are mentioned together. He then clarifies the meaning of 'on the basis of connection': either 'on' retains its sense, or it means 'with' (ba'). An objection is raised: ta'liq implies dependence, which is specific to conjunctive conditionals, yet the author later divides conditionals into conjunctive and disjunctive. The answer is that ta'liq here means a link between the two parts, whether by dependence or by opposition, thus covering both types. Al-Anbabi reinforces that this link is a specific binding that distinguishes conditional from categorical.

Key Terms
تَعْلِيق
شَرْطِيَّة
متصل
منفصل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Why does the author broaden the meaning of ta'liq (connection) to include opposition?
A) To include disjunctive conditionals alongside conjunctive ones.
B) Because all conditionals are based on opposition.
C) To exclude categorical propositions from the definition.
D) Because the author made a mistake.

Key Takeaway
The definition of a conditional proposition hinges on a broad notion of connection (ta'liq) that encompasses both dependence and opposition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The author mentions that ta'liq covers even opposition. Give an example of a disjunctive conditional from Islamic law (e.g., 'The aqiqa is either a goat or a sheep') and explain why it fits the definition.

The Six Types of Accidents
Classification of accidents based on attachment cause

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to classify an accident into one of six types based on whether it attaches by essence, part, or an external relation (coextensive, more general, more specific, or separate).

Core Idea
Accidents are six: essence, part, and four external relations.

In logic, understanding how predicates relate to a subject is crucial. This commentary breaks down accidents into six categories based on the cause of their attachment to a thing. By mastering this classification, you can precisely analyze definitions and distinctions in Islamic sciences.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three main attachment causes
The passage states that what attaches to a thing does so either by virtue of its essence (li dhatihi), a part of its essence (li juz'ihi), or something external to it (li amr kharaj).
Essence and part are internal; external is everything else.
2
Subdivide the external cause into four
The external matter relative to the subject can be coextensive (musawin), more general (a'amm), more specific (akhass), or separate (mubayin).
These four cover all possible relations of an external predicate.
3
Count the total types
Adding the two internal (essence, part) to the four external gives six distinct types of accidents.
The text explicitly says 'the accidents are six'.
4
Apply to a concrete example
Take 'human'. 'Rational' attaches by essence, 'animal' by part (genus), 'laughing' is coextensive, 'walking' is more general (not all humans walk), 'biped' is more specific? Actually biped is coextensive? Better to use 'seeing' as more general? Choose clear examples. For practice, assign each type with a real predicate from Islamic sciences.
Ensure each type has a distinct example.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : (عَنْ عَوَارِضِهِ الذَّاتِيَّةِ) المراد بـ «الذَّاتِيَّة»: «ما يلحق الشَّيء لذاته، واحترز عن: «غير الذاتية». وتفصيلُ ذلك : أنَّ العوارض ستَّةُ ؛ لأنَّ ما يَعرِضُ للشَّيء : إمَّا أن يكون عروضه لذاته، أو لجزئه، أو لأمر خارج عنه . والأمرُ الخارج عن المعروض : إمَّا مساو له، أو أعم منه، أو أخصُّ منه، أو مباين. فالثلاثة الأول وهي العارض للذَّات، وللجزء، والمساوي تسمّى: «أعراضاً ذاتية»؛ لاستنادها إلى ذات المعروض:
His statement: 'concerning its essential accidents' — what is meant by 'essential' is 'that which attaches to the thing by virtue of itself', and he excludes 'non-essential'. The detail of that is: the accidents are six; because what attaches to a thing: either its attachment is by virtue of its essence, or by virtue of its part, or by virtue of something external to it. The external matter relative to the subject: either it is coextensive with it, or more general than it, or more specific than it, or separate. So the first three — namely the accidental by virtue of essence, by virtue of part, and the coextensive — are called 'essential accidents' because they are based on the essence of the subject.
Source pages p. 404

Commentary
This passage is an explanation of the essential accident ('arad dhati) mentioned in the matn. The author first defines dhati as what attaches to a thing due to its very essence, excluding non-essential. He then details that accidents (things that occur to a subject) are of six types based on the cause of attachment: either (1) by the subject's essence, (2) by its part, or (3) by something external. The external cases are further divided into (a) coextensive (identical in extension), (b) more general, (c) more specific, or (d) completely separate. The first three types — attachment by essence, by part, and by what is coextensive — are called essential accidents because they ultimately trace back to the subject's essence. For example, in the subject 'human', 'rational' attaches by essence, 'animal' attaches by part (genus), and 'laughing' is coextensive (a property that is coextensive with human). The non-essential accidents are those that are more general, more specific, or separate, like 'walking' (more general? Actually walking is not coextensive but a general accident) etc. The classification is standard in logic.

Key Takeaway
